It is based on the ROYAL IRISH ACADEMY’S Dictionary of the Irish Language based mainly on Old and Middle Irish materials (1913-1976) which covers the period c.700-c.1700. The current site contains revisions to c.4000 entries and further corrections and additions will be added in the coming years.">

<div class="row result"><div class="col-sm-3 result_headword"><h3 headword_id="14787">1 de, di</h3><span class="citation">Cite this: eDIL s.v. 1 de, di or <a class="reference" href="14787">dil.ie/14787</a></span><br/><span class="revision">Last Revised:

          <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Thes." title="eDIL Bibliography">Thes.</strong></span> ii 343. 32
        
      . Under the influence 
of the pron. cpd. de, de became quite a common spelling 
in Mid.-Ir. (LU, LL, <span class="lbl">&amp;c.</span>). As an etymological writing 
frequent also in modern books and MSS. Spoken de, 
dhe = di + suff. pron. <span class="per 3">3</span> <span class="number sg.">sg.</span>, <span class="lbl">cf.</span> mod. aige, ass, air, <span class="lbl">&amp;c.</span>
    </p>
  
  
    <p class="result"> 
di usual Old- and Mid.-Ir. writing.</p>

    <p class="result"> 
 DO for DI. The confusion of di and do in proclisis 
dates back to the Cambr. and the Wb glosses. It is quite 
possible that on the lips of the people di had as early as the 
8th century the same forms as to-day, <span class="lbl">i.e.</span> unstressed də in 
proclisis, dí- in pronominal cpds. Under the influence of 
the latter forms (dím, dít, de), di de lingered on all through 
Mid.-Ir. The present de, dhe, is partly due to etymological 
spelling, partly (Cork, Kerry) renders a spoken de, ye 
= Oir. de (di + <span class="pos pron." style="color: #ff8000">pron.</span> <span class="per 3">3</span> <span class="number sg.">sg.</span>), <span class="lbl">cf.</span> mod. as, air, aige. The fusion of di and do was facilitated by their being unaccented; 
but it was also partly due to syntactical confusion of these 
two prepositions which occasionally might both be used 
synonymously with one and the same verb.—do: 
        prímgeinde do hireschaib,

    <p class="result"> 
MID.-IR. din, den, don (arch. -nd, -nn); before ṡ- dint, 
<span class="lbl">&amp;c.</span> MOD. don, den, dhen, dhent (<span class="lbl">v.</span> col. 128), 
        don t (don 
tṡaoghal, don tṡruth, don tṡluach). <span class="number Plur.">Plur.</span> Mid.-Ir. dina, 
dena, dona. These forms date back to Oir., <span class="lbl">cf.</span> <span class="ref"><a class="normal" href="search?search_in=headword&amp;q=1 do">1 do</a></span>. Mod. 
dona, dena, dhena, Munster dosna (modelled on isna, leisna, 
<span class="lbl">&amp;c.</span>).</p>

MID. &amp; MOD.-IR. <span class="per 1">1</span> <span class="number sg.">sg.</span> dim, dem, dom, <span class="lbl">&amp;c.</span>; mod. dom. 
<span class="per 2">2</span> <span class="number sg.">sg.</span> dit, det, dot; mod. dot, dod. <span class="per 2">2</span> <span class="number pl.">pl.</span> 
        difar n-, 
        dib(h)ar 
n-, 
        dib(h)or n-; do bar n-, <span class="lbl">&amp;c.</span>; mod. do bhur n-, 
        d'úr n-. <span class="per 3">3</span> <span class="number sg.">sg.</span> 
<span class="number pl.">pl.</span> and <span class="per 1">1</span> <span class="number pl.">pl.</span> The frequent early Mid.-Ir. writing dá (<span class="edilb">n</span>-), 
 
        dár n- shows that in the spoken language of the 11th century 
dia had become dá; dia, however, as a literary form lingered 
on for centuries until the close of the Mid.-Ir. period. Mod. 

        dá (n-), 
        dhá (n-); 
        dár n-, 
        dhár n-. For 
        dá (n-) in the 
spoken language very commonly dona, modelled on iona. 
Exx. of dá from the LU: 
        is cumma imthescad dá sciath ┐ 
dá sleg ┐ dá chlaideb, 
          79 b 14
        
      ; 
        <span class="lbl">ib.</span> 27
      . 
        da, 
          <span class="lbl">ib.</span> 36
        
      ; 
        81 a 25
      .

        <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/QuigginVorton.W&#xF6;rter" title="eDIL Bibliography">Quiggin Vorton. Wörter</strong></span> 35
      .

(de- rare in this sense, <span class="lbl">cf.</span> de-ḟordal, der-).</p>
   
    <p class="result"> 
 ORIGIN. Italo-Celt. <span class="frn ">dē</span>, Ir. dí. In accented position 
also dĕ. The latter possibly includes the particle dis-, 
Lat. <span class="frn ">dis-</span>. Compare especially the distinction made between 
dí-chor <span class="ediltr">a putting away</span> and de-chor <span class="ediltr">a disagreement, 
a difference, diversity</span>, and that between dí-taa <span class="ediltr">stands apart</span> 
and de-buith <span class="ediltr">difference of opinion, dissent</span>. De- from dis- 
possibly also in dedail. De- was either developed before 
broad consonants or possibly like Gk. <span class="frn ">δία</span> it goes back to 
dis-a (modelled on <span class="frn ">μητά, κατά</span> = Ir. ceta-). As dĕ- in the 
great majority of cases has the same functions as dí-, this 
explanation presupposes a blending of dē and dis in Irish. 
The original sense of dis- seems preserved only in nominal 
cpds. in Irish (de-chor, de-buith, de-dail).</p>
   
    <p class="result"> 
 OMISSION OF DI. Di is frequently omitted before the 
second of two substantives connected by ocus or nó: 
        di 
ael ┐ chrieid, 
          <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Ml." title="eDIL Bibliography">Ml.</strong></span> 102 d 9
        
      .

      .

      . 

        <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/MacCarthy" title="eDIL Bibliography">MacCarthy</strong></span>, 62. 8 <span class="lbl">f.b.</span>
       
        nech dia chlaind nó huib, 
          <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/RC" title="eDIL Bibliography">RC</strong></span> xxiv 
202. 17
        
      .— Repeated: 
        <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Wb." title="eDIL Bibliography">Wb.</strong></span> 13 d 7
      , 
        25 c 12
      . 
        <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Ml." title="eDIL Bibliography">Ml.</strong></span> 67 b 17

    <p class="result"> 
 
 SENSES. Di corresponds in usage closely to Lat. <span class="frn ">de</span>, 
which it constantly translates in the Glosses, and with which 
it was identified by Irish grammarians: optimi de se meriti, 
gl. 
        díb, 
          <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Ml." title="eDIL Bibliography">Ml.</strong></span> 61 b 17
        
      . de uita certamen, gl. 
        din bethaid

        dinaib

      .

      ,

12

        59

        115
      . 
        132
      . 
        140
      . 
        <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Thes." title="eDIL Bibliography">Thes.</strong></span> ii 26. 40
      , <span class="lbl">&amp;c.</span>— 
        <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Ml." title="eDIL Bibliography">Ml.</strong></span> 
27 d 12
       it is introduced to render Lat. <span class="frn ">dis-</span> in vb. cpds.: 

non dissimulat, gl. ní deintamladar. Like Lat. <span class="frn ">de</span> it does 
not usually apply to time, except in a few petrified phrases, 
such as di chéin, do ló, d'adaig, d'aidchi, <span class="lbl">&amp;c.</span>, <span class="lbl">cf.</span> XLIX 
below. It unites the senses of the original ablative, 
instrumental and genitive.</p>
  
  
    <p class="result"> 
De, di in a privative and emphatic sense (Lat. <span class="frn ">di-magis</span>), 
see above.</p>

        <p class="result"> 
 <span class="edilb">XXVIII</span> The idiom attá di <span class="ediltr">such is</span> originally meant <span class="trans">it 
<span class="def">follows</span>, <span class="def">comes</span>, <span class="def">results</span> from</span> (<span class="ref"><a class="normal" href="search?search_in=headword&amp;q=de XXVII (b)">XXVII (b)</a></span> <span class="lbl">supra</span>), <span class="trans">
            <span class="def">is because of</span>
          </span>. 
A similar usage occurs with do-ic: dothicfad dia nimut 
nách biath anóir forro = <span class="ediltr">such would be their abundance that 
they would not be valued</span> 
            <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/ZCP" title="eDIL Bibliography">ZCP</strong></span> i 386. 10
          ; and with rosaig: 
rossaig do binne a labartha conid, <span class="lbl">&amp;c.</span>, 
            <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/TBith." title="eDIL Bibliography">TBith.</strong></span> 101
          . —For the 
sense-development <span class="lbl">cf.</span> especially: consternatus sum, gl. 
            atá di 
thrummai na fochaide insin, 
              <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Ml." title="eDIL Bibliography">Ml.</strong></span> 23 a 19
            
          , which might be 
translated: <span class="ediltr">this results from the heaviness of the tribulations</span>. robói du méit a pecthae som combu uisse a nepeltu 
i nóinecht <span class="ediltr">so great were their sins that</span>...(orig. it followed 
from the greatness of their sins, <span class="lbl">&amp;c.</span>), 
            <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Ml." title="eDIL Bibliography">Ml.</strong></span> 98 c 6
          .

              <span class="lbl">ib.</span>

      <p class="result"> 
 
 <span class="edilb">B</span> In the construction of certain verbs (and subst.) which 
might partly belong to the categories mentioned above.</p>
      

        <p class="result"> 
 <span class="edilb">XXXII</span> aithnid, Mid.-Ir. aichnid, <span class="trans">
            <span class="def">enjoins</span>, <span class="def">commands</span>
          </span>: 
            ná 
derb le maith ná le saith issed aithnius díot an Flaith, 
              <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/ZCP" title="eDIL Bibliography">ZCP</strong></span> vii 
301 §8
            
          . 
            tre forchongra in Tailgind do aichin dim dul ann, 

              <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Acall." title="eDIL Bibliography">Acall.</strong></span> 7254
            
          . mar daithin díobh gan <span class="ediltr">where he commanded 
them not</span>, <span class="lbl">&amp;c.</span>, Keat. 
            aithnes dia apstalaib, 
              <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/LU" title="eDIL Bibliography">LU</strong></span> 35 a 31
            
          . 
            dá 
ḟeadhnachaib, 
              <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/ML" title="eDIL Bibliography">ML</strong></span> 64. 22
            
          . 
            roaithniged díob, 
              <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/SG" title="eDIL Bibliography">SG</strong></span> 54. 21
            
          .— 
One would expect do (<span class="lbl">cf.</span> 
            <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Cog." title="eDIL Bibliography">Cog.</strong></span> 146. 16
          ); di seems to have 
arisen in such phrases as do aithin dóib (díb) cen...do dénum.

      <p class="result"> 
 
 <span class="edilb">C</span> DI IN A PARTITIVE SENSE, often taking the place of 
the old genitive.—The partitive sense arose when di loosened 
its connection with the verb and became dependent on some 
neighbouring word denoting quantity. Compare the following instances where the sense of the original ablative 
is still apparent: assibsem imróol di rath in spirito <span class="ediltr">we have 
drunk a great draught of</span> (orig. <span class="ediltr">from) the grace of the Spirit</span> 

          <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Wb." title="eDIL Bibliography">Wb.</strong></span> 12 a 17
        . 
          <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Ml." title="eDIL Bibliography">Ml.</strong></span> 94 c 12
        . 
          cach æn ibius ní din tobur, 
            <span><strong class="pop" style="color:blue" href="bibliography/Acall." title="eDIL Bibliography">Acall.</strong></span> 
6778
          
         (<span class="lbl">cf.</span> 
          ité atoibet do thopur na deachta,

        ). gatar

        <p class="result"> 
 <span class="edilb">XXXVI</span> GENETIVUS GENERIS: after a collective, quantitative or numeral word, di indicates the constituent units 
or substance. Competing with the genitive and sometimes 
not distinguishable from the gen. materiae (<span class="ref"><a class="normal" href="search?search_in=headword&amp;q=de XXIX">XXIX</a></span> above) 
and partitivus.</p>

also <span class="ref"><a class="normal" href="search?search_in=headword&amp;q=d&#xE1;irithe">dáirithe</a></span>.</p>

<script>

	  $(document).ready( function() {

	  	$('#content').on('click', '.pop', function() {
	  		$.ajax({
	            context: this,  // set the context of the callbacks
	            type: "GET",
	            url:  $(this).attr('href'),
	            success: function(data) {
	            	$(this).popover({
	            		placement: function (context, source) {
					        var position = $(source).position();
					        if (position.left > 515) {
					            return "left";
					        }
					        if (position.left < 515) {
					            return "right";
					        }
					        if (position.top < 110){
					            return "bottom";
					        }
					        return "top";
						},
				        content: data,
				        title: 'Bibliography',
				        html: true,
	            	}).popover('show');
	            }
        	});
	  	});

		$('body').on('click', function (e) {
            $('.pop').each(function () {
                //the 'is' for buttons that trigger popups
                //the 'has' for icons within a button that triggers a popup
                if (!$(this).is(e.target) && $(this).has(e.target).length === 0 && $('.popover').has(e.target).length === 0) {
                    $(this).popover('destroy');
                }
            });
        });
	  })
</script>

		max-width: 350px;
